sites, it is not strictly necessary to check all of\\n179: // these relationships (for example, for forward convolution, we compute\\n180: // the size of output ourselves, so we don't actually need to check\\n181: // output.  However, writing a single function that does everything\\n182: // means we get to reuse it for both forwards and all backwards\\n183: // variants, even when the set of \\\"real\\\" inputs varies.  The magic of\\n184: // relational computing!\\n185: //\\n186: // (There is one downside, which is that it is slightly harder to write\\n187: // error messages which are able to distinguish between real inputs\\n188: // (which the user can change) and computed inputs (which the user can\\n189: // only indirectly affect). don't actually need to check\n181: // output.  However, writing a single function that does everything\n182: // means we get to reuse it for both forwards and all backwards\n183: // variants, even when the set of \"real\" inputs varies. However, writing a single function that does everything\n// means we get to reuse it for both forwards and all backwards\n// variants, even when the set of \"real\" inputs varies.  The magic of\n// relational computing!\n//\n// (There is one downside, which is that it is slightly harder to write\n// error messages which are able to distinguish between real inputs\n// (which the user can change) and computed inputs (which the user can\n// only indirectly affect).
